The Hanafi Ruling: No Combination of Stoning and Flogging for Muhsan
Ruling with proof from Prophetic practice and rational reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a muhsan adulterer and identify the two proofs from the source text.

Core Idea
A muhsan receives only stoning; flogging is not combined because the Prophet never did so and it serves no purpose.

In this passage, the author presents the Hanafi position on the punishment for a muhsan – one who qualifies for stoning. The ruling is clear: only stoning, no flogging. The proof is both from Prophetic practice (fi‘l) and rational reasoning (ma‘qul). Let us examine the arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The text states that for the muhsan (محصن), stoning and flogging are not combined (لا يجمع). The ruling is that only stoning is applied.
Muhsan definition: free, adult, married Muslim with valid consummation.
2
Locate the first proof: Prophetic practice
The author argues: 'because the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not combine them' (لأنه عليه السلام لم يجمع). This refers to the known cases of Ma‘iz and the woman of Ghamid.
The absence of combining in the Prophet's practice indicates it is not part of the Shari‘a.
3
Locate the second proof: rational purpose
Flogging is void of purpose with stoning (الجلد يعرى عن المقصود مع الرجم). Why? Because stoning is the ultimate punishment (أقصاها) for deterring others, and the offender himself cannot be deterred after death.
This is a teleological (maqasid) argument: the purpose is deterrence, and stoning achieves it fully.
4
Note the conditions for ihsan (implied)
Although not explicitly stated in this passage, the Hanafi school requires both spouses to be free, Muslim, adult, sane, and to have consummated a valid marriage. This is derived from other texts.
Intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf says a Muslim man can be muhsan by a Kitabiyya; the relied-upon position says no.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَا يُجْمَعُ فِي الْمُحْصَنِ بَيْنَ الرَّجْمِ وَالْجَلْدِ، لِأَنَّهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لَمْ يَجْمَعْ، وَلِأَنَّ الْجَلْدَ يُعْرَى عَنِ الْمَقْصُودِ مَعَ الرَّجْمِ، لِأَنَّ زَجْرَ غَيْرِهِ يَحْصُلُ بِالرَّجْمِ إِذْ هُوَ فِي الْعُقُوبَةِ أَقْصَاهَا، وَزَجْرُهُ لَا يَحْصُلُ بَعْدَ هَلَاكِهِ.
He said: And for the muhsan, stoning and flogging are not combined, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not combine them, and because flogging is void of purpose with stoning, since deterrence of others is achieved by stoning as it is the ultimate punishment, and his own deterrence is not achieved after his death.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling that a muhsan (a free, adult, married Muslim who has consummated marriage) adulterer receives only the punishment of stoning (rajm), not both stoning and flogging. The proof is twofold: first, the Prophet (peace be upon him) never combined both punishments in any known case; second, flogging after stoning is pointless because stoning already serves the purpose of deterring others (being the harshest penalty) and the offender is beyond deterrence after death. The text mentions that Imam Malik, al-Shafi‘i, and in one narration Ahmad ibn Hanbal combine both punishments based on the hadith of ‘Ubadah ibn al-Samit. However, the Hanafi position is that this hadith is abrogated or was specific to the initial revelation of the verse. The commentary also clarifies that the conditions for ihsan include that both spouses be free, Muslim, adult, sane, and that consummation occurred in a valid marriage. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that a Muslim man becomes muhsan by marriage to a Jewish or Christian woman, but the relied-upon position (that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that he does not, as the hadith states a Muslim is not made muhsan by a non-Muslim woman. Similarly, a free man is not made muhsan by a slave wife, nor a free woman by a slave husband.

Key Terms
محصن (muhsan): A free, adult, married Muslim who has consummated the marriage; liable for stoning (rajm) for zina.
رجم (rajm): The punishment of stoning to death for a muhsan who commits zina.
جلد (jald): Flogging with a whip; the hadd punishment for a non-muhsan (bikr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a reason the Hanafi school does not combine stoning and flogging for a muhsan?
A) Stoning is not an effective deterrent
B) The Prophet never combined them
C) Flogging is more severe than stoning
D) The Quran only mentions flogging

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that a muhsan receives only stoning because the Prophet never combined punishments and flogging after stoning is pointless.

Hudud — Fixed Penalties
advanced
10 min
The Inter-Madhhab Dispute: Combining Stoning and Flogging for Muhsan
Inter-madhhab dispute over combining hudud punishments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the inter-madhhab dispute on combining punishments for a muhsan and evaluate the Hanafi response against the Shafi‘i argument.

Core Idea
The Shafi‘i claim to combine punishments is abrogated by the Prophet’s later practice that only stoned muhsan offenders.

While the Hanafi school is certain that a muhsan receives only stoning, other schools—especially the Shafi‘i—argue for combining stoning with flogging. This lesson explores the proof of the opposing school and the Hanafi rebuttal based on the principle of abrogation (naskh) and rational purpose.

Guided Path
1
State the opposing position
Imam al-Shafi‘i (and Malik, and Ahmad in one narration) hold that a muhsan should receive both 100 lashes and stoning. Their primary evidence is the hadith of ‘Ubadah ibn al-Samit.
This hadith is recorded in many collections, including Muslim.
2
Examine the opposing proof
The hadith states: 'The virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile; the thayyib with the thayyib: one hundred lashes and stoning.' The term 'thayyib' here is synonymous with muhsan.
The apparent meaning of the text supports combination.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school argues that this hadith is abrogated (mansukh) by the later practice of the Prophet, who only stoned muhsan offenders in the known cases (Ma‘iz, Ghamid woman). Additionally, the rational argument that flogging is purposeless after stoning reinforces the abrogation.
Abrogation is established because the later practice contradicts the earlier statement, and the later action takes precedence.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: In Hanafi courts, a muhsan is only stoned; in Shafi‘i courts, he is flogged and then stoned.
This affects the implementation of hadd in Muslim jurisdictions.
5
Determine the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi because the later Prophetic practice overrides the earlier hadith via naskh, and it is consistent with the maqasid of the Shari‘a (purpose of punishment: deterrence and retribution).
The principle that later practice abrogates earlier statements is a well-established usul rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَا يُجْمَعُ فِي الْمُحْصَنِ بَيْنَ الرَّجْمِ وَالْجَلْدِ، لِأَنَّهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لَمْ يَجْمَعْ، وَلِأَنَّ الْجَلْدَ يُعْرَى عَنِ الْمَقْصُودِ مَعَ الرَّجْمِ، لِأَنَّ زَجْرَ غَيْرِهِ يَحْصُلُ بِالرَّجْمِ إِذْ هُوَ فِي الْعُقُوبَةِ أَقْصَاهَا، وَزَجْرُهُ لَا يَحْصُلُ بَعْدَ هَلَاكِهِ.
He said: And for the muhsan, stoning and flogging are not combined, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not combine them, and because flogging is void of purpose with stoning, since deterrence of others is achieved by stoning as it is the ultimate punishment, and his own deterrence is not achieved after his death.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling that a muhsan (a free, adult, married Muslim who has consummated marriage) adulterer receives only the punishment of stoning (rajm), not both stoning and flogging. The proof is twofold: first, the Prophet (peace be upon him) never combined both punishments in any known case; second, flogging after stoning is pointless because stoning already serves the purpose of deterring others (being the harshest penalty) and the offender is beyond deterrence after death. The text mentions that Imam Malik, al-Shafi‘i, and in one narration Ahmad ibn Hanbal combine both punishments based on the hadith of ‘Ubadah ibn al-Samit. However, the Hanafi position is that this hadith is abrogated or was specific to the initial revelation of the verse. The commentary also clarifies that the conditions for ihsan include that both spouses be free, Muslim, adult, sane, and that consummation occurred in a valid marriage. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that a Muslim man becomes muhsan by marriage to a Jewish or Christian woman, but the relied-upon position (that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that he does not, as the hadith states a Muslim is not made muhsan by a non-Muslim woman. Similarly, a free man is not made muhsan by a slave wife, nor a free woman by a slave husband.

Key Terms
محصن (muhsan): A free, adult, married Muslim who has consummated the marriage; liable for stoning (rajm) for zina.
رجم (rajm): The punishment of stoning to death for a muhsan who commits zina.
جلد (jald): Flogging with a whip; the hadd punishment for a non-muhsan (bikr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of the hadith that says 'the thayyib gets 100 lashes and stoning'?
A) It is authentic and should be applied
B) It is abrogated by later practice
C) It is a weak hadith
D) It applies only to virgins

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school abrogates the explicit hadith of combining punishments by citing the later Prophetic practice and the purposelessness of flogging after death.

No Combination of Flogging and Exile for the Bikr
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on flogging and exile

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on flogging and exile for the bikr, explain the Hanafi dalil from Quran and the abrogation argument, and compare it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
For a bikr, flogging is the only hadd; exile is ta'zir, not part of the fixed punishment.

The hadd for zina distinguishes between married (muhsan) and unmarried (bikr) offenders. While all schools agree on flogging for the bikr, a dispute exists over whether exile is also a mandatory hadd punishment. In this passage from al-Hidayah, al-Marghinani presents the Hanafi view that exile is not a hadd but a discretionary ta'zir, and provides the proof for this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (zina committed by a bikr) and what the Hanafi school states as the fixed punishment (hadd). The passage clearly states: 'لا يجمع في البكر بين الجلد والنفي' – flogging and exile are not combined as hadd.
The term 'bikr' here means a free, adult, unmarried Muslim who has never consummated marriage.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is the Quranic verse (24:2): 'فاجلدوا كل واحد منهما مائة جلدة' (so flog each of them one hundred lashes). The imperative 'fajlidu' indicates that flogging is the complete obligation. Adding exile would be an addition to the text.
The letter 'fa' (ثم) in the Arabic syntax is often used to indicate a following consequence, but here the Hanafi argue it limits the obligation to what is mentioned.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi reasoning: the verse mentions only flogging, so exile cannot be a hadd because it would be an addition to the clear text. Adding to a clear text is equivalent to abrogation, which cannot be done by an ahad hadith.
The Shafi'i rely on a hadith: 'البكر بالبكر جلد مائة وتغريب عام' – the Hanafi response is that this hadith is abrogated.
4
Follow the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response
The Shafi'i school combines flogging and exile based on the hadith. The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) the hadith is abrogated (just like the part prescribing stoning for the muhsan was abrogated), and (2) exile can have negative social consequences – it removes shame, cuts off livelihood, and may force the offender into a life of prostitution.
The Hanafi also cite the saying of 'Ali (رضي الله عنه): 'كفى بالنفي فتنة' – exile is enough as a trial.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: Shafi'i schools apply exile as part of the hadd (must be combined). The Hanafi school applies only flogging as hadd, but permits the imam to exile as a ta'zir punishment if he sees benefit. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.
The term 'ta'zir' indicates a discretionary punishment left to the ruler's judgment, not fixed by divine text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَا يُجْمَعُ فِي الْبِكْرِ بَيْنَ الْجَلْدِ وَالنَّفْيِ، وَالشَّافِعِيُّ يَجْمَعُ بَيْنَهُمَا حَدًّا لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ: الْبِكْرُ بِالْبِكْرِ جَلْدُ مِائَةٍ وَتَغْرِيبُ عَامٍ، وَلِأَنَّ فِيهِ حَسْمَ بَابِ الزِّنَا لِقِلَّةِ الْمَعَارِفِ. وَلَنَا قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَىٰ: فَاجْلِدُوا، جُعِلَ الْجَلْدُ كُلَّ الْوَاجِبِ رُجُوعًا إِلَى حَرْفِ الْقَاءِ أَوْ إِلَى كَوْنِهِ كُلَّ الْمَذْكُورِ. وَلِأَنَّ فِي التَّغْرِيبِ فَتْحَ بَابِ الزِّنَا لِانْعِدَامِ الِاسْتِحْيَاءِ مِنَ الْعَشِيرَةِ، ثُمَّ فِيهِ قَطْعُ مَوَادِّ الْبَقَاءِ، فَرُبَّمَا تَتَّخِذُ الزِّنَا حِرْفَةً وَهُوَ مِنْ أَقْبَحِ وُجُوهِ الزِّنَا، وَهَذِهِ الْجِهَةُ مُرَجَّحَةٌ لِقَوْلِ عَلِيٍّ: كَفَى بِالنَّفْيِ فِتْنَةً. وَالْحَدِيثُ مَنْسُوخٌ كَشَطْرِهِ، وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ: وَرَجْمًا بِالْحِجَارَةِ. وَقَدْ عُرِفَ طَرِيقُهُ فِي مَوْضِعِهِ. إِلَّا أَنْ يَرَى الْإِمَامُ فِي ذَلِكَ مَصْلَحَةً فَيُغَرِّبَهُ عَلَى قَدْرِ مَا يَرَى، وَذَلِكَ تَعْزِيرٌ وَسِيَاسَةٌ، لِأَنَّهُ قَدْ يُفِيدُ فِي بَعْضِ الْأَحْوَالِ، فَيَكُونُ الرَّأْيُ فِيهِ إِلَى الْإِمَامِ. وَعَلَيْهِ يُحْمَلُ النَّفْيُ الْمَرْوِيُّ عَنْ بَعْضِ الصَّحَابَةِ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمْ.
He said: And for the bikr, flogging and exile are not combined. Al-Shafi‘i combines them as a hadd, based on his (peace be upon him) saying: 'The virgin with the virgin: flogging one hundred and exile for a year,' and because it cuts off the door of zina due to scarcity of acquaintances. Our proof is His statement: 'Flog them' (24:2), making flogging the entire obligation, referring to the letter fa or to its being all that is mentioned. And because in exile there is opening the door of zina due to loss of shyness from the tribe, and cutting off means of livelihood, so perhaps she would take zina as a profession, which is among the ugliest forms of zina. This aspect is strengthened by the saying of ‘Ali: 'Exile is sufficient as a trial.' The hadith is abrogated like part of it, namely his saying: 'and stoning with stones,' and its method is known in its place. However, if the imam sees a benefit in it, he may exile him to the extent he sees fit, and that is ta‘zir and policy, for it may benefit in some situations; thus the opinion in it is left to the imam. And upon this is the reported exile from some Companions (may Allah be pleased with them all) interpreted.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage provides the Hanafi ruling that for a bikr (a free, unmarried, adult Muslim who has not consummated marriage) the hadd punishment for zina is only flogging (100 lashes); exile (taghrib, banishment for one year) is not part of the hadd. The Shafi‘i school disagrees, combining both punishments as a hadd based on the hadith: 'The virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for a year.' The Hanafi response is twofold. First, the Qur’anic verse (An-Nur 24:2) uses the imperative 'fajlidu' (so flog them), and the word 'fa' indicates that flogging is the complete obligatory punishment; adding exile would be an addition to the text, which is tantamount to abrogation (naskh), and the Qur’an cannot be abrogated by an ahad hadith. Second, exile itself can be counterproductive: it removes the offender from the protective shame of his tribe, cuts off his livelihood, and may force him to make zina his profession, which is even worse. Since flogging alone fulfills the purpose of deterrence and reform, exile is not needed as a hadd. However, the Hanafis do not totally reject exile; they classify it as a ta‘zir (discretionary punishment) that the imam may impose if he sees a public benefit. The reports of the Prophet and the Companions (like Ibn ‘Umar, Abu Bakr, and ‘Umar) exiling offenders are interpreted as ta‘zir, not as hadd. The hadith used by al-Shafi‘i is considered abrogated (mansukh) like its other part that also prescribed flogging for muhsan, which was later replaced by stoning alone.

Key Terms
بكر (bikr): A free, adult, unmarried Muslim who has not consummated marriage; liable for flogging (100 lashes) for zina.
نفي (nafy): Banishment from the city; the Hanafi school does not consider it a hadd but a ta‘zir punishment.
تعزير (ta‘zir): A discretionary punishment determined by the ruler for offenses that do not have a fixed hadd or when the hadd conditions are not fully met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is exile not part of the fixed hadd for a bikr?
A) Because the Qur’an only mentions flogging, and adding to it would be abrogation.
B) Because exile was never practiced by the Prophet or Companions.
C) Because the bikr must be sentenced by a judge, not by the ruler.
D) Because exile is a punishment only for married offenders.

Key Takeaway
For the bikr, only flogging is the hadd; exile is not part of the fixed punishment but may be imposed as ta'zir at the imam's discretion.
